<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Polish-French physicist and chemist (1867–1934)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Polish-French physicist. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Marie_Curie_(disambiguation)" title="Marie Curie (disambiguation)">Marie Curie (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the musician, see <a href="/wiki/Marie_Currie" title="Marie Currie">Marie Currie</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">In this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slavic_name" title="Slavic name">Slavic name</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">surname</a> is <i> Skłodowska</i>, sometimes transliterated as <i> Sklodowska</i>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Warszawa,_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4003" data-file-width="5996" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Warszawa%2C_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg/260px-Warszawa%2C_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Warszawa%2C_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg/390px-Warszawa%2C_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Warszawa%2C_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg/520px-Warszawa%2C_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Warszawa,_ul._Freta_16_20170516_002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Maria_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie_Museum" title="Maria Skłodowska-Curie Museum">Birthplace</a>, <i>ulica Freta</i> 16, <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Marie Salomea Skłodowska–Curie</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ʊər/: 'our' in 'tour'">ʊər</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">KURE</span>-ee</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <small>French pronunciation: ​</small><span class="IPA" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[maʁi kyʁi]</a></span>, <small>Polish pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" lang="pl-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish" title="Help:IPA/Polish">[ˈmarja skwɔˈdɔfska kʲiˈri]</a></span>; born <b>Maria Salomea Skłodowska</b>, <small>Polish: </small><span class="IPA" lang="pl-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish" title="Help:IPA/Polish">[ˈmarja salɔˈmɛa skwɔˈdɔfska]</a></span>; 7 November 1867 – 4 July 1934) was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_minority_in_France" title="Polish minority in France">Polish and naturalized-French</a> <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> who conducted pioneering research on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a>. She was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_female_Nobel_laureates" title="List of female Nobel laureates">first woman</a> to win a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a>, the first person and the only woman to <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize#Multiple_laureates" title="Nobel Prize">win the Nobel Prize twice</a>, and the only person to win the Nobel Prize in two scientific fields. Her husband, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a>, was a co-winner on her first Nobel Prize, making them the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize#Statistics" title="Nobel Prize">first ever married couple</a> to win the Nobel Prize and launching the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize#Family_laureates" title="Nobel Prize">Curie family legacy</a> of five Nobel Prizes. She was, in 1906, the first woman to become a professor at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madame_Curie's_Passion_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madame_Curie's_Passion-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>She was born in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, in what was then the <a href="/wiki/Congress_Poland" title="Congress Poland">Kingdom of Poland</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>. She studied at Warsaw's clandestine <a href="/wiki/Flying_University" title="Flying University">Flying University</a> and began her practical scientific training in Warsaw. In 1891, aged 24, she followed her elder sister <a href="/wiki/Bronis%C5%82awa_D%C5%82uska" title="Bronisława Dłuska">Bronisława</a> to study in Paris, where she earned her higher degrees and conducted her subsequent scientific work. In 1895 she married the French physicist <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a>, and she shared the 1903 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> with him and with the physicist <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Henri Becquerel</a> for their pioneering work developing the theory of "radioactivity"—a term she coined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1906 Pierre Curie died in a Paris street accident. Marie won the 1911 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> for her discovery of the elements <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>, using techniques she invented for isolating radioactive <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>.
</p><p>Under her direction, the world's first studies were conducted into the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Neoplasm" title="Neoplasm">neoplasms</a> by the use of radioactive isotopes. In 1920 she founded the <a href="/wiki/Curie_Institute_(Paris)" title="Curie Institute (Paris)">Curie Institute in Paris</a>, and in 1932 the <a href="/wiki/Maria_Sklodowska-Curie_National_Research_Institute_of_Oncology" title="Maria Sklodowska-Curie National Research Institute of Oncology">Curie Institute in Warsaw</a>; both remain major centres of medical research. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> she developed mobile radiography units to provide <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> services to <a href="/wiki/Field_hospital" title="Field hospital">field hospitals</a>.
</p><p>While a French citizen, Marie Skłodowska Curie, who used both surnames,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> never lost her sense of <a href="/wiki/National_identity" title="National identity">Polish identity</a>. She taught her daughters the <a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish language</a> and took them on visits to Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldsmith_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldsmith-10">[10]</a></sup> She named the first <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> she discovered <i>polonium</i>, after her native country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marie Curie died in 1934, aged 66, at the <span title="French-language text"><span lang="fr" style="font-style: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Sancellemoz" title="Sancellemoz">Sancellemoz</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Sanatorium" title="Sanatorium">sanatorium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Passy,_Haute-Savoie" title="Passy, Haute-Savoie">Passy</a> (<span title="French-language text"><span lang="fr" style="font-style: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Haute-Savoie" title="Haute-Savoie">Haute-Savoie</a></span></span>), France, of <a href="/wiki/Aplastic_anemia" title="Aplastic anemia">aplastic anemia</a> from exposure to radiation in the course of her scientific research and in the course of her radiological work at field hospitals during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> In addition to her Nobel Prizes, she has received numerous other honours and tributes; in 1995 she became the first woman to be entombed on her own merits in the Paris <span title="French-language text"><span lang="fr" style="font-style: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on" title="Panthéon">Panthéon</a></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-14">[13]</a></sup> and Poland declared 2011 the Year of Marie Curie during the <a href="/wiki/International_Year_of_Chemistry" title="International Year of Chemistry">International Year of Chemistry</a>. She is the subject of numerous biographical works, where she is also known as <b><span title="French-language text"><span lang="fr" style="font-style: normal;">Madame Curie</span></span></b>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg/170px-Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg/255px-Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg/340px-Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sklodowski_Family_Wladyslaw_and_his_daughters_Maria_Bronislawa_Helena.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Władysław Skłodowski, daughters <i>(from left)</i> Maria, <a href="/wiki/Bronis%C5%82awa_D%C5%82uska" title="Bronisława Dłuska">Bronisława</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helena_Sk%C5%82odowska-Szalay" title="Helena Skłodowska-Szalay">Helena</a>, 1890</div></div></div>
<p>Maria Skłodowska was born in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Congress_Poland" title="Congress Poland">Congress Poland</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>, on 7 November 1867, the fifth and youngest child of well-known teachers Bronisława, <i>née</i> Boguska, and Władysław Skłodowski.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> The elder siblings of Maria (<a href="/wiki/Nickname" title="Nickname">nicknamed</a> <i>Mania</i>) were Zofia (born 1862, nicknamed <i>Zosia</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Sk%C5%82odowski" title="Józef Skłodowski">Józef</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://pl.wikipedia.org/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Sk%C5%82odowski_(lekarz)" title="pl:Józef Skłodowski (lekarz)">pl</a>]</span> (born 1863, nicknamed <i>Józio</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bronis%C5%82awa_D%C5%82uska" title="Bronisława Dłuska">Bronisława</a> (born 1865, nicknamed <i>Bronia</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Helena_Sk%C5%82odowska-Szalay" title="Helena Skłodowska-Szalay">Helena</a> (born 1866, nicknamed <i>Hela</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>On both the paternal and maternal sides, the family had lost their property and fortunes through patriotic involvements in Polish national uprisings aimed at restoring Poland's independence (the most recent had been the <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">January Uprising</a> of 1863–65).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> This condemned the subsequent generation, including Maria and her elder siblings, to a difficult struggle to get ahead in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> Maria's paternal grandfather, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Sk%C5%82odowski" title="Józef Skłodowski">Józef Skłodowski</a>, had been principal of the <a href="/wiki/Lublin" title="Lublin">Lublin</a> primary school attended by <a href="/wiki/Boles%C5%82aw_Prus#Early_years" title="Bolesław Prus">Bolesław Prus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> who became a leading figure in Polish literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-undoubtedly_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-undoubtedly-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Władysław Skłodowski taught mathematics and physics, subjects that Maria was to pursue, and was also director of two Warsaw <i><a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasia</a></i> (secondary schools) for boys. After Russian authorities eliminated laboratory instruction from the Polish schools, he brought much of the laboratory equipment home and instructed his children in its use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup> He was eventually fired by his Russian supervisors for pro-Polish sentiments and forced to take lower-paying posts; the family also lost money on a bad investment and eventually chose to supplement their income by lodging boys in the house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup> Maria's mother Bronisława operated a prestigious Warsaw boarding school for girls; she resigned from the position after Maria was born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup> She died of <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> in May 1878, when Maria was ten years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup> Less than three years earlier, Maria's oldest sibling, Zofia, had died of <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> contracted from a boarder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup> Maria's father was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist" title="Atheist">atheist</a>, her mother a devout Catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barker2011_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barker2011-21">[20]</a></sup> The deaths of Maria's mother and sister caused her to give up Catholicism and become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agnostic" title="Agnostic">agnostic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="442" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg/170px-Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg/255px-Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg/340px-Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maria_Sklodowska_et_sa_s%C5%93ur_Bronislawa_en_1886.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Maria (left), sister <a href="/wiki/Bronis%C5%82awa_D%C5%82uska" title="Bronisława Dłuska">Bronisława</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1886</span></div></div></div>
<p>When she was ten years old, Maria began attending the boarding school of J. Sikorska; next, she attended a <i><a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasium</a></i> for girls, from which she graduated on 12 June 1883 with a gold medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> After a collapse, possibly due to <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">depression</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup> she spent the following year in the countryside with relatives of her father, and the next year with her father in Warsaw, where she did some tutoring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> Unable to enroll in a regular institution of higher education because she was a woman, she and her sister Bronisława became involved with the clandestine <a href="/wiki/Flying_University" title="Flying University">Flying University</a> (sometimes translated as <i>Floating University</i>), a Polish patriotic institution of higher learning that admitted women students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Krakowskie_Przedmiescie,_Warsaw.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2773" data-file-width="1655" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Krakowskie_Przedmiescie%2C_Warsaw.JPG/170px-Krakowskie_Przedmiescie%2C_Warsaw.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Krakowskie_Przedmiescie%2C_Warsaw.JPG/255px-Krakowskie_Przedmiescie%2C_Warsaw.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Krakowskie_Przedmiescie%2C_Warsaw.JPG/340px-Krakowskie_Przedmiescie%2C_Warsaw.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Krakowskie_Przedmiescie,_Warsaw.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krakowskie_Przedmiescie" title="Krakowskie Przedmiescie">Krakowskie Przedmiescie</a></i> 66, <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, where Maria did her first scientific work, 1890–91.</div></div></div>
<p>Maria made an agreement with her sister, Bronisława, that she would give her financial assistance during Bronisława's medical studies in Paris, in exchange for similar assistance two years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> In connection with this, Maria took a position first as a home tutor in Warsaw, then for two years as a <a href="/wiki/Governess" title="Governess">governess</a> in <a href="/wiki/Szczuki,_Masovian_Voivodeship" title="Szczuki, Masovian Voivodeship">Szczuki</a> with a landed family, the Żorawskis, who were relatives of her father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> While working for the latter family, she fell in love with their son, <a href="/wiki/Kazimierz_%C5%BBorawski" title="Kazimierz Żorawski">Kazimierz Żorawski</a>, a future eminent mathematician.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> His parents rejected the idea of his marrying the penniless relative, and Kazimierz was unable to oppose them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> Maria's loss of the relationship with Żorawski was tragic for both. He soon earned a doctorate and pursued an academic career as a mathematician, becoming a professor and <a href="/wiki/Rector_(academia)" title="Rector (academia)">rector</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Kraków University</a>. Still, as an old man and a mathematics professor at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warsaw_Polytechnic" title="Warsaw Polytechnic">Warsaw Polytechnic</a>, he would sit contemplatively before the statue of Maria Skłodowska that had been erected in 1935 before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curie_Institute,_Warsaw" title="Curie Institute, Warsaw">Radium Institute</a>, which she had founded in 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert2_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert2-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the beginning of 1890, Bronisława—who a few months earlier had married <a href="/wiki/Kazimierz_D%C5%82uski" title="Kazimierz Dłuski">Kazimierz Dłuski</a>, a Polish physician and social and political activist—invited Maria to join them in Paris. Maria declined because she could not afford the university tuition; it would take her a year and a half longer to gather the necessary funds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> She was helped by her father, who was able to secure a more lucrative position again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> All that time she continued to <a href="/wiki/Autodidacticism" title="Autodidacticism">educate herself</a>, reading books, exchanging letters, and being tutored herself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> In early 1889 she returned home to her father in Warsaw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> She continued working as a governess and remained there until late 1891.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> She tutored, studied at the Flying University, and began her practical scientific training (1890–91) in a chemical laboratory at the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Industry_and_Agriculture" title="Museum of Industry and Agriculture">Museum of Industry and Agriculture</a> at <i><a href="/wiki/Krakowskie_Przedmie%C5%9Bcie" title="Krakowskie Przedmieście">Krakowskie Przedmieście</a></i> 66, near <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Old_Town" title="Warsaw Old Town">Warsaw's Old Town</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup> The laboratory was run by her cousin <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Boguski" title="Józef Boguski">Józef Boguski</a>, who had been an assistant in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> to the Russian chemist <a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev" title="Dmitri Mendeleev">Dmitri Mendeleev</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1_23-8"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Polish_Girlhood_(1867–1891)1-23">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-teachers_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-teachers-25">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_in_Paris">Life in Paris</span></h3>
<p>In late 1891, she left Poland for France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup> In Paris, Maria (or Marie, as she would be known in France) briefly found shelter with her sister and brother-in-law before renting a <a href="/wiki/Garret" title="Garret">garret</a> closer to the university, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_Quarter" title="Latin Quarter">Latin Quarter</a>, and proceeding with her studies of physics, chemistry, and mathematics at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>, where she enrolled in late 1891.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert3_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert3-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> She subsisted on her meagre resources, keeping herself warm during cold winters by wearing all the clothes she had. She focused so hard on her studies that she sometimes forgot to eat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> Skłodowska studied during the day and tutored evenings, barely earning her keep. In 1893, she was awarded a degree in physics and began work in an industrial laboratory of <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Lippmann" title="Gabriel Lippmann">Gabriel Lippmann</a>. Meanwhile, she continued studying at the University of Paris and with the aid of a fellowship she was able to earn a second degree in 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-10"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[b]</a></sup>
</p><p>Skłodowska had begun her scientific career in Paris with an investigation of the magnetic properties of various steels, commissioned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_for_the_Encouragement_of_National_Industry" title="Society for the Encouragement of National Industry">Society for the Encouragement of National Industry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> That same year, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre Curie</a> entered her life: it was their mutual interest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_sciences" title="Natural sciences">natural sciences</a> that drew them together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams331_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams331-30">[28]</a></sup> Pierre Curie was an instructor at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_City_of_Paris_Industrial_Physics_and_Chemistry_Higher_Educational_Institution" title="The City of Paris Industrial Physics and Chemistry Higher Educational Institution">The City of Paris Industrial Physics and Chemistry Higher Educational Institution</a> (ESPCI Paris).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-12"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> They were introduced by Polish physicist <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B3zef_Wierusz-Kowalski" title="Józef Wierusz-Kowalski">Józef Wierusz-Kowalski</a>, who had learned that she was looking for a larger laboratory space, something that Wierusz-Kowalski thought Pierre could access.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-13"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> Though Curie did not have a large laboratory, he was able to find some space for Skłodowska where she was able to begin work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Their mutual passion for science brought them increasingly closer, and they began to develop feelings for one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-14"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> Eventually, Pierre proposed marriage, but at first Skłodowska did not accept as she was still planning to go back to her native country. Curie, however, declared that he was ready to move with her to Poland, even if it meant being reduced to teaching French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-15"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> Meanwhile, for the 1894 summer break, Skłodowska returned to Warsaw, where she visited her family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> She was still labouring under the illusion that she would be able to work in her chosen field in Poland, but she was denied a place at <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Kraków University</a> because of <a href="/wiki/Sexism_in_academia" title="Sexism in academia">sexism in academia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> A letter from Pierre convinced her to return to Paris to pursue a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ph.D" title="Ph.D">Ph.D</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> At Skłodowska's insistence, Curie had written up his research on <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> and received his own doctorate in March 1895; he was also promoted to professor at the School.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-9"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> A contemporary quip would call Skłodowska "Pierre's biggest discovery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 26 July 1895, they were married in <a href="/wiki/Sceaux,_Hauts-de-Seine" title="Sceaux, Hauts-de-Seine">Sceaux</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> neither wanted a religious service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-16"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-10"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> Curie's dark blue outfit, worn instead of a bridal gown, would serve her for many years as a laboratory outfit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_28-11"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris-28">[27]</a></sup> They shared two pastimes: long bicycle trips and journeys abroad, which brought them even closer. In Pierre, Marie had found a new love, a partner, and a scientific collaborator on whom she could depend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_elements">New elements</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1740" data-file-width="2163" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg/220px-Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg/330px-Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg/440px-Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_and_Marie_Curie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre</a> and Marie Curie in the laboratory, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1904</div></div></div>
<p>In 1895, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_R%C3%B6ntgen" title="Wilhelm Röntgen">Wilhelm Röntgen</a> discovered the existence of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a>, though the mechanism behind their production was not yet understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup> In 1896, <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Henri Becquerel</a> discovered that <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> salts emitted rays that resembled X-rays in their penetrating power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup> He demonstrated that this radiation, unlike <a href="/wiki/Phosphorescence" title="Phosphorescence">phosphorescence</a>, did not depend on an external source of energy but seemed to arise spontaneously from uranium itself. Influenced by these two important discoveries, Curie decided to look into uranium rays as a possible field of research for a thesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-17"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>She used an innovative technique to investigate samples. Fifteen years earlier, her husband and his brother had developed a version of the <a href="/wiki/Electrometer" title="Electrometer">electrometer</a>, a sensitive device for measuring electric charge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup> Using her husband's electrometer, she discovered that uranium rays caused the air around a sample to conduct electricity. Using this technique, her first result was the finding that the activity of the uranium compounds depended only on the quantity of uranium present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup> She <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesized</a> that the radiation was not the outcome of some interaction of <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> but must come from the <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup> This hypothesis was an important step in disproving the assumption that atoms were indivisible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert4_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert4-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1897, her daughter <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène</a> was born. To support her family, Curie began teaching at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Normale_Sup%C3%A9rieure" title="École Normale Supérieure">École Normale Supérieure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup> The Curies did not have a dedicated laboratory; most of their research was carried out in a converted shed next to ESPCI.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup> The shed, formerly a medical school dissecting room, was poorly ventilated and not even waterproof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup> They were unaware of the deleterious effects of <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">radiation exposure</a> attendant on their continued unprotected work with radioactive substances. ESPCI did not sponsor her research, but she would receive subsidies from metallurgical and mining companies and from various organizations and governments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_2-35">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Curie's systematic studies included two uranium minerals, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitchblende" title="Pitchblende">pitchblende</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torbernite" title="Torbernite">torbernite</a> (also known as chalcolite).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup> Her electrometer showed that pitchblende was four times as active as uranium itself, and chalcolite twice as active. She concluded that, if her earlier results relating the quantity of uranium to its activity were correct, then these two minerals must contain small quantities of another substance that was far more active than uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert5_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert5-36">[34]</a></sup> She began a systematic search for additional substances that emit radiation, and by 1898 she discovered that the element <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> was also radioactive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity-32">[30]</a></sup> Pierre Curie was increasingly intrigued by her work. By mid-1898 he was so invested in it that he decided to drop his work on crystals and to join her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The [research] idea [writes Reid] was her own; no one helped her formulate it, and although she took it to her husband for his opinion she clearly established her ownership of it. She later recorded the fact twice in her biography of her husband to ensure there was no chance whatever of any ambiguity. It [is] likely that already at this early stage of her career [she] realized that... many scientists would find it difficult to believe that a woman could be capable of the original work in which she was involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert6_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert6-37">[35]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="612" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg/220px-Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg/330px-Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg/440px-Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Pierre_Irene_Curie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène</a>, &amp; Marie Curie, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1902</div></div></div>
<p>She was acutely aware of the importance of promptly publishing her discoveries and thus establishing her <a href="/wiki/Scientific_priority" title="Scientific priority">priority</a>. Had not Becquerel, two years earlier, presented his discovery to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences" title="Académie des Sciences">Académie des Sciences</a></i> the day after he made it, credit for the discovery of radioactivity (and even a Nobel Prize), would instead have gone to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silvanus_Thompson" title="Silvanus Thompson">Silvanus Thompson</a>. Curie chose the same rapid means of publication. Her paper, giving a brief and simple account of her work, was presented for her to the <i>Académie</i> on 12 April 1898 by her former professor, <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Lippmann" title="Gabriel Lippmann">Gabriel Lippmann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert7_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert7-38">[36]</a></sup> Even so, just as Thompson had been beaten by Becquerel, so Curie was beaten in the race to tell of her discovery that thorium gives off rays in the same way as uranium; two months earlier, <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Carl_Schmidt" title="Gerhard Carl Schmidt">Gerhard Carl Schmidt</a> had published his own finding in Berlin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robert_Reid_p._65_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_Reid_p._65-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>At that time, no one else in the world of physics had noticed what Curie recorded in a sentence of her paper, describing how much greater were the activities of pitchblende and chalcolite than uranium itself: "The fact is very remarkable, and leads to the belief that these minerals may contain an element which is much more active than uranium." She later would recall how she felt "a passionate desire to verify this hypothesis as rapidly as possible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robert_Reid_p._65_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_Reid_p._65-39">[37]</a></sup> On 14 April 1898, the Curies optimistically weighed out a 100-gram sample of pitchblende and ground it with a pestle and mortar. They did not realize at the time that what they were searching for was present in such minute quantities that they would eventually have to process tonnes of the ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robert_Reid_p._65_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_Reid_p._65-39">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 1898, Curie and her husband published a joint paper announcing the existence of an element they named "<a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a>", in honour of her native Poland, which would for another twenty years remain <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">partitioned among three empires</a> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Empire" title="Austrian Empire">Austrian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Prussian</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-18"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup> On 26 December 1898, the Curies announced the existence of a second element, which they named "<a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>", from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word for "ray".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Discovery_of_Radioactivity_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Discovery_of_Radioactivity-40">[38]</a></sup> In the course of their research, they also coined the word "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-19"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Curie_(1859-1906)_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_(1867-1934),_c._1903_(4405627519).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2547" data-file-width="2255" decoding="async" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pierre_Curie_%281859-1906%29_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_%281867-1934%29%2C_c._1903_%284405627519%29.jpg/220px-Pierre_Curie_%281859-1906%29_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_%281867-1934%29%2C_c._1903_%284405627519%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pierre_Curie_%281859-1906%29_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_%281867-1934%29%2C_c._1903_%284405627519%29.jpg/330px-Pierre_Curie_%281859-1906%29_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_%281867-1934%29%2C_c._1903_%284405627519%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Pierre_Curie_%281859-1906%29_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_%281867-1934%29%2C_c._1903_%284405627519%29.jpg/440px-Pierre_Curie_%281859-1906%29_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_%281867-1934%29%2C_c._1903_%284405627519%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Curie_(1859-1906)_and_Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_(1867-1934),_c._1903_(4405627519).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pierre and Marie Curie, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span class="nowrap"> </span>1903</div></div></div>
<p>To prove their discoveries beyond any doubt, the Curies sought to isolate polonium and radium in pure form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-6"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup> Pitchblende is a complex mineral; the chemical separation of its constituents was an arduous task. The discovery of polonium had been relatively easy; chemically it resembles the element <a href="/wiki/Bismuth" title="Bismuth">bismuth</a>, and polonium was the only bismuth-like substance in the ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-7"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup> Radium, however, was more elusive; it is closely related chemically to <a href="/wiki/Barium" title="Barium">barium</a>, and pitchblende contains both elements. By 1898 the Curies had obtained traces of radium, but appreciable quantities, uncontaminated with barium, were still beyond reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-williams-41">[39]</a></sup> The Curies undertook the arduous task of separating out radium salt by differential <a href="/wiki/Crystallization" title="Crystallization">crystallization</a>. From a tonne of pitchblende, one-tenth of a gram of <a href="/wiki/Radium_chloride" title="Radium chloride">radium chloride</a> was separated in 1902. In 1910, she isolated pure radium metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-8"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams332_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams332-42">[40]</a></sup> She never succeeded in isolating polonium, which has a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of only 138 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-9"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1898 and 1902, the Curies published, jointly or separately, a total of 32 scientific papers, including one that announced that, when exposed to <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>, diseased, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor" title="Tumor">tumour-forming</a> cells were destroyed faster than healthy cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1900, Curie became the first woman faculty member at the École Normale Supérieure and her husband joined the faculty of the University of Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinn1996_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn1996-45">[43]</a></sup> In 1902 she visited Poland on the occasion of her father's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 1903, supervised by <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Lippmann" title="Gabriel Lippmann">Gabriel Lippmann</a>, Curie was awarded her doctorate from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_discovery_of_radium_in_1898_by_Maria_Sklodowska-Curie_(1867–1934)_and_Pierre_Curie_(1859–1906)_with_commentary_on_their_life_and_times_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_discovery_of_radium_in_1898_by_Maria_Sklodowska-Curie_(1867–1934)_and_Pierre_Curie_(1859–1906)_with_commentary_on_their_life_and_times-46">[44]</a></sup> That month the couple were invited to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institution" title="Royal Institution">Royal Institution</a> in London to give a speech on radioactivity; being a woman, she was prevented from speaking, and Pierre Curie alone was allowed to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis-47">[45]</a></sup> Meanwhile, a new industry began developing, based on radium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3-44">[42]</a></sup> The Curies did not <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> their discovery and benefited little from this increasingly profitable business.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2_34-10"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_2-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nobel_Prizes">Nobel Prizes</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_1903.jpg"><img alt="Polnische Frauen, Polnische Frau, Polish women, Polish Woman" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="280" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg/170px-Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg/255px-Marie_Curie_1903.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Marie_Curie_1903.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_1903.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1903 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> portrait</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6253" data-file-width="9277" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg/170px-Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg/255px-Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg/340px-Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nobel_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1903 Nobel Prize diploma</div></div></div>
<p>In December 1903 the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences</a> awarded Pierre Curie, Marie Curie, and Henri Becquerel the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a>, "in recognition of the extraordinary services they have rendered by their joint researches on the <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation" title="Ionizing radiation">radiation</a> phenomena discovered by Professor Henri Becquerel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-8"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup> At first the committee had intended to honour only Pierre Curie and Henri Becquerel, but a committee member and advocate for women scientists, Swedish mathematician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnus_G%C3%B6sta_Mittag-Leffler" title="Magnus Gösta Mittag-Leffler">Magnus Gösta Mittag-Leffler</a>, alerted Pierre to the situation, and after his complaint, Marie's name was added to the nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2-48">[46]</a></sup> Marie Curie was the first woman to be awarded a Nobel Prize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-9"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Curie and her husband declined to go to <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a> to receive the prize in person; they were too busy with their work, and Pierre Curie, who disliked public ceremonies, was feeling increasingly ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis-47">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2-48">[46]</a></sup> As Nobel laureates were required to deliver a lecture, the Curies finally undertook the trip in 1905.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2-48">[46]</a></sup> The award money allowed the Curies to hire their first laboratory assistant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2-48">[46]</a></sup> Following the award of the Nobel Prize, and galvanized by an offer from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Geneva" title="University of Geneva">University of Geneva</a>, which offered Pierre Curie a position, the University of Paris gave him a professorship and the chair of physics, although the Curies still did not have a proper laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-10"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_and_the_Science_of_Radioactivity_3-44">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinn1996_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quinn1996-45">[43]</a></sup> Upon Pierre Curie's complaint, the University of Paris relented and agreed to furnish a new laboratory, but it would not be ready until 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2-48">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="408" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg/170px-Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg/255px-Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg/340px-Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_Vanity_Fair_1904-12-22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Caricature of Marie and Pierre Curie, captioned "Radium", in the London magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(British_magazine)" title="Vanity Fair (British magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i>, December 1904</div></div></div>
<p>In December 1904, Curie gave birth to their second daughter, <a href="/wiki/%C3%88ve_Curie" title="Ève Curie">Ève</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2_48-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Rec_and_Dis2-48">[46]</a></sup> She hired Polish governesses to teach her daughters her native language, and sent or took them on visits to Poland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldsmith_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-goldsmith-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 19 April 1906, Pierre Curie was killed in a road accident. Walking across the <a href="/wiki/Rue_Dauphine" title="Rue Dauphine">Rue Dauphine</a> in heavy rain, he was struck by a <a href="/wiki/Horse-drawn_vehicle" title="Horse-drawn vehicle">horse-drawn vehicle</a> and fell under its wheels, fracturing his skull and killing him instantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-11"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obit_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-obit-49">[47]</a></sup> Curie was devastated by her husband's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad-50">[48]</a></sup> On 13 May 1906 the physics department of the University of Paris decided to retain the chair that had been created for her late husband and offer it to Marie. She accepted it, hoping to create a world-class laboratory as a tribute to her husband Pierre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad-50">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad2_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad2-51">[49]</a></sup> She was the first woman to become a professor at the University of Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-12"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Curie's quest to create a new laboratory did not end with the University of Paris, however. In her later years, she headed the Radium Institute (<i>Institut du radium</i>, now <a href="/wiki/Curie_Institute_(Paris)" title="Curie Institute (Paris)">Curie Institute</a>, <i>Institut Curie</i>), a radioactivity laboratory created for her by the <a href="/wiki/Pasteur_Institute" title="Pasteur Institute">Pasteur Institute</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad2_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad2-51">[49]</a></sup> The initiative for creating the Radium Institute had come in 1909 from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pierre_Paul_%C3%89mile_Roux" title="Pierre Paul Émile Roux">Pierre Paul Émile Roux</a>, director of the Pasteur Institute, who had been disappointed that the University of Paris was not giving Curie a proper laboratory and had suggested that she move to the Pasteur Institute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-13"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> Only then, with the threat of Curie leaving, did the University of Paris relent, and eventually the Curie Pavilion became a joint initiative of the University of Paris and the Pasteur Institute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1911_Solvay_conference.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1362" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1911_Solvay_conference.jpg/220px-1911_Solvay_conference.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1911_Solvay_conference.jpg/330px-1911_Solvay_conference.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/1911_Solvay_conference.jpg/440px-1911_Solvay_conference.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1911_Solvay_conference.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>At the first <a href="/wiki/Solvay_Conference" title="Solvay Conference">Solvay Conference</a> (1911), Curie (seated, second from right) confers with <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincaré">Henri Poincaré</a>; standing nearby are <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Rutherford</a> (fourth from right), <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a> (second from right), and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Langevin" title="Paul Langevin">Paul Langevin</a> (far right).</div></div></div>
<p>In 1910 Curie succeeded in isolating radium; she also defined an international standard for radioactive emissions that was eventually named for her and Pierre: the <a href="/wiki/Curie_(unit)" title="Curie (unit)">curie</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad2_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Tr_and_Ad2-51">[49]</a></sup> Nevertheless, in 1911 the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a> failed, by one<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-14"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup> or two votes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec-53">[51]</a></sup> to elect her to membership in the academy. Elected instead was <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Branly" title="Édouard Branly">Édouard Branly</a>, an inventor who had helped <a href="/wiki/Guglielmo_Marconi" title="Guglielmo Marconi">Guglielmo Marconi</a> develop the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraph" title="Wireless telegraph">wireless telegraph</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldsmith9_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldsmith9-54">[52]</a></sup> It was only over half a century later, in 1962, that a doctoral student of Curie's, <a href="/wiki/Marguerite_Perey" title="Marguerite Perey">Marguerite Perey</a>, became the first woman elected to membership in the academy.
</p><p>Despite Curie's fame as a scientist working for France, the public's attitude tended toward <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>—the same that had led to the <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus_affair" title="Dreyfus affair">Dreyfus affair</a>—which also fuelled false speculation that Curie was Jewish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-15"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec-53">[51]</a></sup> During the French Academy of Sciences elections, she was vilified by the right-wing press as a foreigner and atheist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec-53">[51]</a></sup> Her daughter later remarked on the French press's hypocrisy in portraying Curie as an unworthy foreigner when she was nominated for a French honour, but portraying her as a French heroine when she received foreign honours such as her Nobel Prizes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-16"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1911 it was revealed that Curie was involved in a year-long affair with physicist <a href="/wiki/Paul_Langevin" title="Paul Langevin">Paul Langevin</a>, a former student of Pierre Curie's,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert10_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert10-55">[53]</a></sup> a married man who was estranged from his wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec-53">[51]</a></sup> This resulted in a press scandal that was exploited by her academic opponents. Curie (then in her mid-40s) was five years older than Langevin and was misrepresented in the tabloids as a foreign Jewish home-wrecker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldsmith11_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldsmith11-56">[54]</a></sup> When the scandal broke, she was away at a conference in Belgium; on her return, she found an angry mob in front of her house and had to seek refuge, with her daughters, in the home of her friend, <a href="/wiki/Camille_Marbo" title="Camille Marbo">Camille Marbo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1508" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg/220px-Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg/330px-Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg/440px-Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie%27s_Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry_1911.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1911 Nobel Prize diploma</div></div></div>
<p>International recognition for her work had been growing to new heights, and the Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences, overcoming opposition prompted by the Langevin scandal, honoured her a second time, with the 1911 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> This award was "in recognition of her services to the advancement of chemistry by the discovery of the elements radium and polonium, by the isolation of radium and the study of the nature and compounds of this remarkable element."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2-57">[55]</a></sup> Because of the negative publicity due to her affair with Langevin, the chair of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_committee" title="Nobel committee">Nobel committee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Svante_Arrhenius" title="Svante Arrhenius">Svante Arrhenius</a>, attempted to prevent her attendance at the official ceremony for her Nobel Prize in Chemistry, citing her questionable moral standing. Curie replied that she would be present at the ceremony, because "the prize has been given to her for her discovery of polonium and radium" and that "there is no relation between her scientific work and the facts of her private life".
</p><p>She was the first person to win or share two Nobel Prizes, and remains alone with <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling" title="Linus Pauling">Linus Pauling</a> as Nobel laureates in two fields each. A delegation of celebrated Polish men of learning, headed by novelist <a href="/wiki/Henryk_Sienkiewicz" title="Henryk Sienkiewicz">Henryk Sienkiewicz</a>, encouraged her to return to Poland and continue her research in her native country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> Curie's second Nobel Prize enabled her to persuade the French government to support the Radium Institute, built in 1914, where research was conducted in chemistry, physics, and medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> A month after accepting her 1911 Nobel Prize, she was hospitalised with depression and a kidney ailment. For most of 1912, she avoided public life but did spend time in England with her friend and fellow physicist, <a href="/wiki/Hertha_Ayrton" title="Hertha Ayrton">Hertha Ayrton</a>. She returned to her laboratory only in December, after a break of about 14 months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1912 the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Scientific_Society" title="Warsaw Scientific Society">Warsaw Scientific Society</a> offered her the directorship of a new laboratory in Warsaw but she declined, focusing on the developing Radium Institute to be completed in August 1914, and on a new street named Rue Pierre-Curie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2-57">[55]</a></sup> She was appointed Director of the Curie Laboratory in the Radium Institute of the University of Paris, founded in 1914.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> She visited Poland in 1913 and was welcomed in Warsaw but the visit was mostly ignored by the Russian authorities. The institute's development was interrupted by the coming war, as most researchers were drafted into the <a href="/wiki/French_Army" title="French Army">French Army</a>, and it fully resumed its activities in 1919.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_Sc_and_Rec2-57">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War-59">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="767" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg/220px-Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg/330px-Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg/440px-Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marie_Curie_-_Mobile_X-Ray-Unit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Curie in a mobile X-ray vehicle, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1915</span></div></div></div>
<p>During World War I, Curie recognised that wounded soldiers were best served if operated upon as soon as possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coppes-Zantinga,_A._R_1998_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coppes-Zantinga,_A._R_1998-60">[58]</a></sup> She saw a need for field radiological centres near the front lines to assist battlefield surgeons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War-59">[57]</a></sup> including to obviate amputations when in fact limbs could be saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup> After a quick study of radiology, anatomy, and automotive mechanics she procured X-ray equipment, vehicles, auxiliary generators, and developed mobile <a href="/wiki/Radiography" title="Radiography">radiography</a> units, which came to be popularly known as <i>petites Curies</i> ("Little Curies").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War-59">[57]</a></sup> She became the director of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Cross" title="Red Cross">Red Cross</a> Radiology Service and set up France's first military radiology centre, operational by late 1914.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War-59">[57]</a></sup> Assisted at first by a military doctor and her 17-year-old daughter <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène</a>, Curie directed the installation of 20 mobile radiological vehicles and another 200 radiological units at field hospitals in the first year of the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War_59-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War-59">[57]</a></sup> Later, she began training other women as aides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War2_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War2-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1915, Curie produced hollow needles containing "radium emanation", a colourless, radioactive gas given off by radium, later identified as <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>, to be used for sterilizing infected tissue. She provided the radium from her own one-gram supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War2_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War2-63">[61]</a></sup> It is estimated that over a million wounded soldiers were treated with her X-ray units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> Busy with this work, she carried out very little scientific research during that period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-7"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> In spite of all her humanitarian contributions to the French war effort, Curie never received any formal recognition of it from the French government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War_59-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War-59">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Also, promptly after the war started, she attempted to donate her gold Nobel Prize medals to the war effort but the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banque_de_France" title="Banque de France">French National Bank</a> refused to accept them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War2_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War2-63">[61]</a></sup>  She did buy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_bonds" title="War bonds">war bonds</a>, using her Nobel Prize money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War2_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War2-63">[61]</a></sup> She said:<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I am going to give up the little gold I possess. I shall add to this the scientific medals, which are quite useless to me. There is something else: by sheer laziness I had allowed the money for my second Nobel Prize to remain in Stockholm in Swedish crowns. This is the chief part of what we possess. I should like to bring it back here and invest it in war loans. The state needs it. Only, I have no illusions: this money will probably be lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coppes-Zantinga,_A._R_1998_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Coppes-Zantinga,_A._R_1998-60">[58]</a></sup> </p></blockquote><p>She was also an active member in committees of <a href="/wiki/Poles_in_France" title="Poles in France">Polonia in France</a> dedicated to the Polish cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emigracja_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-emigracja-64">[62]</a></sup> After the war, she summarized her wartime experiences in a book, <i>Radiology in War</i> (1919).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War2_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War2-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar_years">Postwar years</span></h3>
