<p>In 1920, for the 25th anniversary of the discovery of radium, the French government established a stipend for her; its previous recipient was <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a> (1822–95).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-8"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> In 1921, she was welcomed triumphantly when she toured the United States to raise funds for research on radium. <a href="/wiki/Marie_Mattingly_Meloney" title="Marie Mattingly Meloney">Mrs. William Brown Meloney</a>, after interviewing Curie, created a <i>Marie Curie Radium Fund</i> and raised money to buy radium, publicising her trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-9"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_in_America,_1921_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Sklodowska_Curie_in_America,_1921-65">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[c]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1921, U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> received her at the White House to present her with the 1 gram of radium collected in the United States, and the First Lady praised her as an example of a professional achiever who was also a supportive wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madame_Curie's_Passion_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Madame_Curie's_Passion-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute-68">[65]</a></sup> Before the meeting, recognising her growing fame abroad, and embarrassed by the fact that she had no French official distinctions to wear in public, the French government offered her a <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Legion of Honour</a> award, but she refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pasachoff1996_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pasachoff1996-69">[66]</a></sup> In 1922 she became a fellow of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Medicine" title="French Academy of Medicine">French Academy of Medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-10"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> She also travelled to other countries, appearing publicly and giving lectures in Belgium, Brazil, Spain, and Czechoslovakia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amudeu_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-amudeu-70">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2904" data-file-width="2532" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg/170px-Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg/255px-Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg/340px-Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Irene_and_Marie_Curie_1925.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marie and daughter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Curie" title="Irène Curie">Irène</a>, 1925</div></div></div>
<p>Led by Curie, the Institute produced four more Nobel Prize winners, including her daughter <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène Joliot-Curie</a> and her son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric Joliot-Curie</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_2_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute_2-71">[68]</a></sup> Eventually it became one of the world's four major radioactivity-research laboratories, the others being the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Laboratory" title="Cavendish Laboratory">Cavendish Laboratory</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Radium_Research,_Vienna" title="Institute for Radium Research, Vienna">Institute for Radium Research, Vienna</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Meyer_(physicist)" title="Stefan Meyer (physicist)">Stefan Meyer</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Institute_for_Chemistry" title="Max Planck Institute for Chemistry">Kaiser Wilhelm Institute for Chemistry</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_2_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute_2-71">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chemistry_International –_Newsmagazine_for_IUPAC_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chemistry_International_–_Newsmagazine_for_IUPAC-72">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 1922 Marie Curie became a member of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>' newly created <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_on_Intellectual_Cooperation" title="International Committee on Intellectual Cooperation">International Committee on Intellectual Cooperation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-14">[13]</a></sup> She sat on the committee until 1934 and contributed to League of Nations' scientific coordination with other prominent researchers such as <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">Hendrik Lorentz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> In 1923 she wrote a biography of her late husband, titled <i>Pierre Curie</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leg_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-leg-75">[72]</a></sup> In 1925 she visited Poland to participate in a ceremony laying the foundations for Warsaw's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curie_Institute,_Warsaw" title="Curie Institute, Warsaw">Radium Institute</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-11"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> Her second American tour, in 1929, succeeded in equipping the Warsaw Radium Institute with radium; the Institute opened in 1932, with her sister Bronisława its director.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-12"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute-68">[65]</a></sup> These distractions from her scientific labours, and the attendant publicity, caused her much discomfort but provided resources for her work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute-68">[65]</a></sup> In 1930 she was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Isotopic_Abundances_and_Atomic_Weights" title="Commission on Isotopic Abundances and Atomic Weights">International Atomic Weights Committee</a>, on which she served until her death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> In 1931, Curie was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Cameron_Prize_for_Therapeutics_of_the_University_of_Edinburgh" title="Cameron Prize for Therapeutics of the University of Edinburgh">Cameron Prize for Therapeutics of the University of Edinburgh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sklodowska-Curie_statue,_Warsaw.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sklodowska-Curie_statue%2C_Warsaw.JPG/170px-Sklodowska-Curie_statue%2C_Warsaw.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sklodowska-Curie_statue%2C_Warsaw.JPG/255px-Sklodowska-Curie_statue%2C_Warsaw.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Sklodowska-Curie_statue%2C_Warsaw.JPG/340px-Sklodowska-Curie_statue%2C_Warsaw.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sklodowska-Curie_statue,_Warsaw.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1935 statue, facing the Radium Institute, <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a></div></div></div>
<p>Curie visited Poland for the last time in early 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-8"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_3_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute_3-78">[75]</a></sup> A few months later, on 4 July 1934, she died aged 66 at the <a href="/wiki/Sancellemoz" title="Sancellemoz">Sancellemoz</a> sanatorium in <a href="/wiki/Passy,_Haute-Savoie" title="Passy, Haute-Savoie">Passy, Haute-Savoie</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Aplastic_anemia" title="Aplastic anemia">aplastic anemia</a> believed to have been contracted from her long-term exposure to radiation, causing damage to her bone marrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-13"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iuniverse_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-iuniverse-79">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>The damaging effects of ionising radiation were not known at the time of her work, which had been carried out without the safety measures later developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_3_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute_3-78">[75]</a></sup> She had carried test tubes containing radioactive isotopes in her pocket,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShipmanWilson2012_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShipmanWilson2012-80">[77]</a></sup> and she stored them in her desk drawer, remarking on the <a href="/wiki/Radioluminescence" title="Radioluminescence">faint light</a> that the substances gave off in the dark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Vertigo_Years:_Europe,_1900–1914_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Vertigo_Years:_Europe,_1900–1914-81">[78]</a></sup> Curie was also exposed to X-rays from unshielded equipment while serving as a radiologist in field hospitals during the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_War2_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_War2-63">[61]</a></sup>  In fact, when Curie's body was exhumed in 1995, the French <i>Office de Protection contre les Rayonnements Ionisants</i> (<i>ORPI</i>) "concluded that she could not have been exposed to lethal levels of radium while she was alive". They pointed out that radium poses a risk only if it is ingested,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> and speculated that her illness was more likely to have been due to her use of radiography during the First World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>She was interred at the cemetery in <a href="/wiki/Sceaux,_Hauts-de-Seine" title="Sceaux, Hauts-de-Seine">Sceaux</a>, alongside her husband Pierre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb113_52-14"><a href="#cite_note-psb113-52">[50]</a></sup> Sixty years later, in 1995, in honour of their achievements, the remains of both were transferred to the Paris <a href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on" title="Panthéon">Panthéon</a>. Their remains were sealed in a <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> lining because of the radioactivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bi-2015_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-bi-2015-84">[81]</a></sup> She became the second woman to be interred at the  Panthéon (after <a href="/wiki/Sophie_Berthelot" title="Sophie Berthelot">Sophie Berthelot</a>) and the first woman to be honoured with interment in the Panthéon on her own merits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of their levels of radioactive contamination, her papers from the 1890s are considered too dangerous to handle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> Even her cookbooks are highly radioactive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-everything_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-everything-86">[83]</a></sup> Her papers are kept in lead-lined boxes, and those who wish to consult them must wear protective clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-everything_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-everything-86">[83]</a></sup> In her last year, she worked on a book, <i>Radioactivity</i>, which was published posthumously in 1935.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_3_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute_3-78">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg/170px-Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg/255px-Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg/340px-Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lublin_UMCS_Pomnik_Marii_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowskiej.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie_Monument_in_Lublin" title="Marie Curie Monument in Lublin">Marie Curie Monument in Lublin</a></div></div></div><p>
The physical and societal aspects of the Curies' work contributed to shaping the world of the twentieth and twenty-first centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb114_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-psb114-87">[84]</a></sup> Cornell University professor <a href="/wiki/L._Pearce_Williams" title="L. Pearce Williams"><span class="nowrap">L. Pearce</span> Williams</a> observes:<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The result of the Curies' work was epoch-making. Radium's radioactivity was so great that it could not be ignored. It seemed to contradict the principle of the conservation of energy and therefore forced a reconsideration of the foundations of physics. On the experimental level the discovery of radium provided men like Ernest Rutherford with sources of radioactivity with which they could probe the structure of the atom. As a result of Rutherford's experiments with alpha radiation, the nuclear atom was first postulated. In medicine, the radioactivity of radium appeared to offer a means by which cancer could be successfully attacked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams332_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams332-42">[40]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>If Curie's work helped overturn established ideas in physics and chemistry, it has had an equally profound effect in the societal sphere. To attain her scientific achievements, she had to overcome barriers, in both her native and her adoptive country, that were placed in her way because she was a woman. This aspect of her life and career is highlighted in <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Giroud" title="Françoise Giroud">Françoise Giroud</a>'s <i>Marie Curie: A Life</i>, which emphasizes Curie's role as a feminist precursor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-9"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>She was known for her honesty and moderate lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-17"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb114_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-psb114-87">[84]</a></sup> Having received a small scholarship in 1893, she returned it in 1897 as soon as she began earning her keep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-20"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_2_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_–_Student_in_Paris_2-35">[33]</a></sup> She gave much of her first Nobel Prize money to friends, family, students, and research associates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-10"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> In an unusual decision, Curie intentionally refrained from patenting the radium-isolation process so that the scientific community could do research unhindered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robert8_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-robert8-88">[85]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[d]</a></sup> She insisted that monetary gifts and awards be given to the scientific institutions she was affiliated with rather than to her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb114_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-psb114-87">[84]</a></sup> She and her husband often refused awards and medals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-18"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> reportedly remarked that she was probably the only person who could not be corrupted by fame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-11"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours_and_tributes">Honours and tributes</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG/170px-Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG/255px-Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG/340px-Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Panth%C3%A9on_Pierre_et_Marie_Curie.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre</a> and Marie Curie, <a href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on" title="Panthéon">Panthéon</a>, Paris in 2011</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CERN,_Marie_Curie,_Ginebra,_Suiza,_2015_19.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/CERN%2C_Marie_Curie%2C_Ginebra%2C_Suiza%2C_2015_19.JPG/170px-CERN%2C_Marie_Curie%2C_Ginebra%2C_Suiza%2C_2015_19.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/CERN%2C_Marie_Curie%2C_Ginebra%2C_Suiza%2C_2015_19.JPG/255px-CERN%2C_Marie_Curie%2C_Ginebra%2C_Suiza%2C_2015_19.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/CERN%2C_Marie_Curie%2C_Ginebra%2C_Suiza%2C_2015_19.JPG/340px-CERN%2C_Marie_Curie%2C_Ginebra%2C_Suiza%2C_2015_19.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CERN,_Marie_Curie,_Ginebra,_Suiza,_2015_19.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of "Maria Skłodowska-Curie", <a href="/wiki/Microcosm_(CERN)" title="Microcosm (CERN)">CERN Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, 2015</div></div></div>
<p>As one of the most famous scientists, Marie Curie has become an icon in the scientific world and has received tributes from across the globe, even in the realm of <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">pop culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borzendowski2009-36_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borzendowski2009-36-90">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, she became the first woman to be entombed on her own merits in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panth%C3%A9on,_Paris" title="Panthéon, Paris">Panthéon, Paris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a 2009 poll carried out by <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i>, she was voted the "most inspirational woman in science". Curie received 25.1 percent of all votes cast, nearly twice as many as second-place <a href="/wiki/Rosalind_Franklin" title="Rosalind Franklin">Rosalind Franklin</a> (14.2 per cent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Most_inspirational_woman_scientist_revealed_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Most_inspirational_woman_scientist_revealed-91">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie_voted_greatest_female_scientist_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie_voted_greatest_female_scientist-92">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the centenary of her second Nobel Prize, Poland declared 2011 the Year of Marie Curie;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Hindu_2010_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Hindu_2010-93">[89]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> declared that this would be the <a href="/wiki/International_Year_of_Chemistry" title="International Year of Chemistry">International Year of Chemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosm_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosm-94">[90]</a></sup> An artistic installation celebrating "Madame Curie" filled the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jacobs_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jacobs Gallery (page does not exist)">Jacobs Gallery</a> at <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Contemporary_Art_San_Diego" title="Museum of Contemporary Art San Diego">Museum of Contemporary Art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madame_Curie_art_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madame_Curie_art-95">[91]</a></sup> On 7 November, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> celebrated the anniversary of her birth with a special <a href="/wiki/Google_Doodle" title="Google Doodle">Google Doodle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marie_Curie's_144th_Birthday_Google_Doodle_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marie_Curie's_144th_Birthday_Google_Doodle-96">[92]</a></sup> On 10 December, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Academy_of_Sciences" title="New York Academy of Sciences">New York Academy of Sciences</a> celebrated the centenary of Marie Curie's second <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Princess_Madeleine,_Duchess_of_H%C3%A4lsingland_and_G%C3%A4strikland" title="Princess Madeleine, Duchess of Hälsingland and Gästrikland">Princess Madeleine of Sweden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Princess_Madeleine_attends_celebrations_to_mark_anniversary_of_Marie_Curie's_second_Nobel_Prize_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Princess_Madeleine_attends_celebrations_to_mark_anniversary_of_Marie_Curie's_second_Nobel_Prize-97">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marie Curie was the first woman to win a Nobel Prize, the first person to win two Nobel Prizes, the only woman to win in two fields, and the only person to win in <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize#Multiple_laureates" title="Nobel Prize">multiple sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel_Prize_Facts_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel_Prize_Facts-98">[94]</a></sup> Awards that she received include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> (1903, with her husband Pierre Curie and Henri Becquerel)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb112_26-19"><a href="#cite_note-psb112-26">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Davy_Medal" title="Davy Medal">Davy Medal</a> (1903, with Pierre)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-amudeu_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-amudeu-70">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CurieSheean1999_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-CurieSheean1999-99">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Matteucci_Medal" title="Matteucci Medal">Matteucci Medal</a> (1904, with Pierre)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CurieSheean1999_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-CurieSheean1999-99">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Actonian_Prize" title="Actonian Prize">Actonian Prize</a> (1907)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Elliott_Cresson_Medal" title="Elliott Cresson Medal">Elliott Cresson Medal</a> (1909)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-winners_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-winners-101">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (1911)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-12"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Medal_(American_Philosophical_Society)" title="Benjamin Franklin Medal (American Philosophical Society)">Franklin Medal</a> of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a> (1921)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minutes_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minutes-102">[98]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>She received numerous honorary degrees from universities across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Radium_Institute_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-The_Radium_Institute-68">[65]</a></sup> In Poland, she received honorary doctorates from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lw%C3%B3w_Polytechnic" title="Lwów Polytechnic">Lwów Polytechnic</a> (1912),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pozna%C5%84_University" title="Poznań University">Poznań University</a> (1922), <a href="/wiki/Krak%C3%B3w" title="Kraków">Kraków</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Jagiellonian_University" title="Jagiellonian University">Jagiellonian University</a> (1924), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warsaw_Polytechnic" title="Warsaw Polytechnic">Warsaw Polytechnic</a> (1926).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosm_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-cosm-94">[90]</a></sup>  In 1920 she became the first female member of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Danish_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Letters" title="Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters">The Royal Danish Academy of Sciences and Letters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup> In 1921, in the U.S., she was awarded membership in the <a href="/wiki/Iota_Sigma_Pi" title="Iota Sigma Pi">Iota Sigma Pi</a> women scientists' society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Awards_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Awards-105">[101]</a></sup> In 1924, she became an Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/Polish_Chemical_Society" title="Polish Chemical Society">Polish Chemical Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup> Marie Curie's 1898 publication with her husband and their collaborator <a href="/wiki/Gustave_B%C3%A9mont" title="Gustave Bémont">Gustave Bémont</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paper_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paper-107">[103]</a></sup> of their discovery of radium and polonium was honoured by a Citation for Chemical Breakthrough Award from the Division of History of Chemistry of the American Chemical Society presented to the ESPCI Paris in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Award_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Award-108">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Breakthrough_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Breakthrough-109">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>Entities that have been named in her honour include:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Curie_(unit)" title="Curie (unit)">curie</a> (symbol <b>Ci</b>), a unit of radioactivity, is named in honour of her and Pierre Curie (although the commission which agreed on the name never clearly stated whether the standard was named after Pierre, Marie, or both).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_the_Curie_Came_to_Be_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-How_the_Curie_Came_to_Be-110">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>The element with atomic number 96 was named <a href="/wiki/Curium" title="Curium">curium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curium_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curium-111">[107]</a></sup></li>
<li>Three radioactive minerals are also named after the Curies: <a href="/wiki/Curite" title="Curite">curite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sklodowskite" title="Sklodowskite">sklodowskite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cuprosklodowskite" title="Cuprosklodowskite">cuprosklodowskite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borzendowski2009-37_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Borzendowski2009-37-112">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie_Actions" title="Marie Skłodowska-Curie Actions">Marie Skłodowska-Curie Actions</a> fellowship program of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> for young scientists wishing to work in a foreign country is named after her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_et_Marie_Curie_(Paris_M%C3%A9tro)" title="Pierre et Marie Curie (Paris Métro)">a metro station in Paris</a> was renamed to honour both of the Curies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borzendowski2009-37_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Borzendowski2009-37-112">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li>Polish nuclear research <a href="/wiki/Maria_reactor" title="Maria reactor">reactor Maria</a> is named after her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA_–_reaktor_Maria_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA_–_reaktor_Maria-114">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/7000_Curie" title="7000 Curie">7000 Curie</a> asteroid is also named after her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borzendowski2009-37_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-Borzendowski2009-37-112">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/KLM" title="KLM">KLM</a> <a href="/wiki/McDonnell_Douglas_MD-11" title="McDonnell Douglas MD-11">McDonnell Douglas MD-11</a> (registration PH-KCC) is named in her honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Picture_of_the_McDonnell_Douglas_MD-11_aircraft_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Picture_of_the_McDonnell_Douglas_MD-11_aircraft-115">[111]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2011, a new <a href="/wiki/Maria_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie_Bridge,_Warsaw" title="Maria Skłodowska-Curie Bridge, Warsaw">Warsaw bridge</a> over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vistula_River" title="Vistula River">Vistula River</a> was named in her honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup></li>
<li>In January 2020, <a href="/wiki/Satellogic" title="Satellogic">Satellogic</a>, a high-resolution <a href="/wiki/Earth_observation" title="Earth observation">Earth observation</a> imaging and analytics company, launched a <a href="/wiki/%C3%91uSat" title="ÑuSat">ÑuSat</a> type <a href="/wiki/Small_satellite" title="Small satellite">micro-satellite</a>; ÑuSat 8, also known as Marie, was named in her honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Marie-Curie_station" title="Marie-Curie station">Marie-Curie station</a>, a planned underground <a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9seau_express_m%C3%A9tropolitain" title="Réseau express métropolitain">Réseau express métropolitain</a> (REM) station in the borough of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint-Laurent_(borough)" title="Saint-Laurent (borough)">Saint-Laurent</a> in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> is named in her honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[114]</a></sup>  A nearby road, Avenue Marie Curie, is also named in her honour.</li>
<li>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_docking" title="Molecular docking">molecular docking</a> task  CurieMariedock is a component of the Slovenian <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> project SiDock (which runs under the aegis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BOINC" title="BOINC">BOINC</a>); its focus is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SARS%E2%80%91CoV%E2%80%912" title="SARS‑CoV‑2">SARS‑CoV‑2</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup></li>
<li>Mount Curie in New Zealand's <a href="/wiki/Paparoa_Range" title="Paparoa Range">Paparoa Range</a> was named after her in 1970 by the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Scientific_and_Industrial_Research_(New_Zealand)" title="Department of Scientific and Industrial Research (New Zealand)">Department of Scientific and Industrial Research</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Several institutions presently bear her name, including the two Curie institutes which she founded: the <a href="/wiki/Maria_Sklodowska-Curie_National_Research_Institute_of_Oncology" title="Maria Sklodowska-Curie National Research Institute of Oncology">Maria Sklodowska-Curie National Research Institute of Oncology</a> in Warsaw, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Curie_Institute_(Paris)" title="Curie Institute (Paris)">Institut Curie</a></i> in Paris. The <a href="/wiki/Maria_Curie-Sk%C5%82odowska_University" title="Maria Curie-Skłodowska University">Maria Curie-Skłodowska University</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Lublin" title="Lublin">Lublin</a>, was founded in 1944; and the <a href="/wiki/Pierre_and_Marie_Curie_University" title="Pierre and Marie Curie University">Pierre and Marie Curie University</a> (also known as Paris VI) was France's pre-eminent science university, which would later merge to form the <a href="/wiki/Sorbonne_University" title="Sorbonne University">Sorbonne University</a>. In Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie_(charity)" title="Marie Curie (charity)">Marie Curie charity</a> was organized in 1948 to care for the terminally ill.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup>
Two museums are devoted to Marie Curie. In 1967, the <a href="/wiki/Maria_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie_Museum" title="Maria Skłodowska-Curie Museum">Maria Skłodowska-Curie Museum</a> was established in Warsaw's "<a href="/wiki/Warsaw_New_Town" title="Warsaw New Town">New Town</a>", at her birthplace on <i>ulica Freta</i> (Freta Street).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-13"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> Her Paris laboratory is preserved as the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Curie" title="Musée Curie">Musée Curie</a>, open since 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curie_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-curie-124">[120]</a></sup>
Curie's likeness has appeared on banknotes, stamps and coins around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Borzendowski2009-37_112-3"><a href="#cite_note-Borzendowski2009-37-112">[108]</a></sup> She was featured on the Polish late-1980s 20,000-<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish_zloty" title="Polish zloty">złoty</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">banknote</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Council1997_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Council1997-125">[121]</a></sup> as well as on the last French 500-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%82%A3" title="₣">franc</a> note, before the franc was replaced by the euro.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Letcher2003_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Letcher2003-126">[122]</a></sup> Curie-themed postage stamps from <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Togo" title="Republic of Togo">Republic of Togo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Guinea" title="Republic of Guinea">Republic of Guinea</a> actually show a picture of Susan Marie Frontczak portraying Curie in a 2001 picture by Paul Schroeder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Main,_Douglas_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Main,_Douglas-127">[123]</a></sup>
Her likeness or name has appeared on several artistic works. In 1935, Michalina Mościcka, wife of Polish President <a href="/wiki/Ignacy_Mo%C5%9Bcicki" title="Ignacy Mościcki">Ignacy Mościcki</a>, unveiled a statue of Marie Curie before Warsaw's Radium Institute; during the 1944 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Uprising" title="Warsaw Uprising">Warsaw Uprising</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi German</a> occupation, the monument was damaged by gunfire; after the war it was decided to leave the bullet marks on the statue and its pedestal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gwiazdapolarna_18-14"><a href="#cite_note-gwiazdapolarna-18">[17]</a></sup> Her name is included on the <i><a href="/wiki/Monument_to_the_X-ray_and_Radium_Martyrs_of_All_Nations" title="Monument to the X-ray and Radium Martyrs of All Nations">Monument to the X-ray and Radium Martyrs of All Nations</a></i>, erected in <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, Germany in 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MMI_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-MMI-128">[124]</a></sup> In 1955 <a href="/wiki/Jozef_Mazur" title="Jozef Mazur">Jozef Mazur</a> created a stained glass panel of her, the <a href="/wiki/Maria_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie_Medallion" title="Maria Skłodowska-Curie Medallion">Maria Skłodowska-Curie Medallion</a>, featured in the <a href="/wiki/University_at_Buffalo" title="University at Buffalo">University at Buffalo</a> Polish Room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-buffalo_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-buffalo-129">[125]</a></sup> In 2011, on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centenary" title="Centenary">centenary</a> of Marie Curie's second Nobel Prize, an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> mural was painted on the <a href="/wiki/Fa%C3%A7ade" title="Façade">façade</a> of her Warsaw <a href="/wiki/Maria_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie_Museum" title="Maria Skłodowska-Curie Museum">birthplace</a>. It depicted an infant Maria Skłodowska holding a test tube from which emanated the elements that she would discover as an adult: <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2>
<p>Numerous biographies are devoted to her, including:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/%C3%88ve_Curie" title="Ève Curie">Ève Curie</a> (Marie Curie's daughter), <i>Madame Curie</i>, 1938.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Giroud" title="Françoise Giroud">Françoise Giroud</a>, <i>Marie Curie: A Life</i>, 1987.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Barbara_Goldsmith" title="Barbara Goldsmith">Barbara Goldsmith</a>, <i>Obsessive Genius: The Inner World of Marie Curie</i>, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosm_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-cosm-94">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lauren_Redniss" title="Lauren Redniss">Lauren Redniss</a>, <i>Radioactive: Marie and Pierre Curie, a Tale of Love and Fallout</i>, 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radioactive:_Marie_and_Pierre_Curie,_a_Tale_of_Love_and_Fallout_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radioactive:_Marie_and_Pierre_Curie,_a_Tale_of_Love_and_Fallout-130">[126]</a></sup> adapted into the 2019 British film.</li></ul>
<p>Marie Curie has been the subject of a number of films:
</p>
<ul><li>1943: <i><a href="/wiki/Madame_Curie_(film)" title="Madame Curie (film)">Madame Curie</a></i>, a U.S. Oscar-nominated film by <a href="/wiki/Mervyn_LeRoy" title="Mervyn LeRoy">Mervyn LeRoy</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Greer_Garson" title="Greer Garson">Greer Garson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leg_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-leg-75">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>1997: <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Palmes_de_M._Schutz" title="Les Palmes de M. Schutz">Les Palmes de M. Schutz</a></i>, a French film adapted from a play of the same title, and directed by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Pinoteau" title="Claude Pinoteau">Claude Pinoteau</a>. Marie Curie is played by <a href="/wiki/Isabelle_Huppert" title="Isabelle Huppert">Isabelle Huppert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Les-Palmes-de-M-Schutz_–_Trailer_–_Cast_–_Showtimes_–_NYTimes.com_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Les-Palmes-de-M-Schutz_–_Trailer_–_Cast_–_Showtimes_–_NYTimes.com-131">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li>2014: <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie,_une_femme_sur_le_front" title="Marie Curie, une femme sur le front"><i>Marie Curie, une femme sur le front</i></a>, a French-Belgian film, directed by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alain_Brunard&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alain Brunard (page does not exist)">Alain Brunard</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alain_Brunard" title="fr:Alain Brunard">fr</a>]</span> and starring <a href="/wiki/Dominique_Reymond" title="Dominique Reymond">Dominique Reymond</a>.</li>
<li>2016: <i><a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie:_The_Courage_of_Knowledge" title="Marie Curie: The Courage of Knowledge">Marie Curie: The Courage of Knowledge</a></i>, a European co-production by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Marie_No%C3%ABlle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Marie Noëlle (page does not exist)">Marie Noëlle</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Karolina_Gruszka" title="Karolina Gruszka">Karolina Gruszka</a>.</li>
<li>2019: <i><a href="/wiki/Radioactive_(film)" title="Radioactive (film)">Radioactive</a></i>, a British film by <a href="/wiki/Marjane_Satrapi" title="Marjane Satrapi">Marjane Satrapi</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Rosamund_Pike" title="Rosamund Pike">Rosamund Pike</a>.</li></ul>
<p>Curie is the subject of the 2013 play, <i>False Assumptions</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Aronovitch" title="Lawrence Aronovitch">Lawrence Aronovitch</a>, in which the ghosts of three other women scientists observe events in her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[128]</a></sup> Curie has also been portrayed by Susan Marie Frontczak in her play, <i>Manya: The Living History of Marie Curie</i>, a one-woman show which by 2014 had been performed in 30 U.S. states and nine countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Main,_Douglas_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-Main,_Douglas-127">[123]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Poland had been partitioned in the 18th century among Russia, Prussia, and Austria, and it was Maria Skłodowska Curie's hope that naming the element after her native country would bring world attention to Poland's lack of independence as a sovereign state. <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">Polonium</a> may have been the first chemical element named to highlight a political question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independence_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-independence-11">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources vary concerning the field of her second degree. <a href="/wiki/Tadeusz_Estreicher" title="Tadeusz Estreicher">Tadeusz Estreicher</a>, in the 1938 <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polski_s%C5%82ownik_biograficzny" title="Polski słownik biograficzny">Polski słownik biograficzny</a></i> entry, writes that, while many sources state she earned a degree in mathematics, this is incorrect, and that her second degree was in chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-psb111_15-11"><a href="#cite_note-psb111-15">[14]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Marie Skłodowska Curie was escorted to the United States by the American author and social activist <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Kellogg" title="Charlotte Kellogg">Charlotte Hoffman Kellogg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">However, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">historian of science</a> <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Fara" title="Patricia Fara">Patricia Fara</a> writes: "Marie Skłodowska Curie's reputation as a scientific martyr is often supported by quoting her denial (carefully crafted by her American publicist, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marie_Meloney" title="Marie Meloney">Marie Meloney</a>) that she derived any personal gain from her research: 'There were no patents. We were working in the interests of science. Radium was not to enrich anyone. Radium... belongs to all people.' As Eva Hemmungs Wirtén pointed out in <i>Making Marie Curie</i>, this claim takes on a different hue once you learn that, under French law, Curie was banned from taking out a patent in her own name, so that any profits from her research would automatically have gone to her husband, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Curie" title="Pierre Curie">Pierre</a>." <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Fara" title="Patricia Fara">Patricia Fara</a>, "It leads to everything" (review of Paul Sen, <i>Einstein's Fridge: The Science of Fire, Ice and the Universe</i>, William Collins, April 2021, <style <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiction">Fiction</span></h3>
<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFOlov_Enquist2006">Olov Enquist, Per (2006). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/bookaboutblanche00enqu" rel="nofollow"><i>The Book about Blanche and Marie</i></a>. New York: Overlook. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-58567-668-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-58567-668-2"><bdi>978-1-58567-668-2</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Book+about+Blanche+and+Marie&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Overlook&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-58567-668-2&amp;rft.aulast=Olov+Enquist&amp;rft.aufirst=Per&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fbookaboutblanche00enqu&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMarie+Curie"></span> A 2004 novel by <a href="/wiki/Per_Olov_Enquist" title="Per Olov Enquist">Per Olov Enquist</a> featuring Maria Skłodowska-Curie, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurologist" title="Neurologist">neurologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Martin_Charcot" title="Jean-Martin Charcot">Jean-Martin Charcot</a>, and his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salp%C3%AAtri%C3%A8re" title="Salpêtrière">Salpêtrière</a></i> patient "Blanche" (<a href="/wiki/Marie_Wittman" title="Marie Wittman">Marie Wittman</a>). The English translation was published in 2006.</li></ul>
